<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ded681d1-2953-48e3-962d-c13b277ad21a'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<div id="review_2406521936" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2406521936" />
    <a title="Erin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17957823-erin"><img alt="Erin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597778686p2/17957823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2406521936?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin" href="/user/show/17957823-erin">Erin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17957823-erin?shelf=library-borrowed">library-borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2406521936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9875580241553863810"><i>There she weaves by night and day <br/>A magic web with colours gay. <br/>She has heard a whisper say, <br/>A curse is on her if she stay <br/> To look down to Camelot. <br/>She knows not what the curse may be, <br/>And so she weaveth steadily, <br/>And little other care hath she, <br/> The Lady of Shalott. </i> Alfred, Lord Tennyson <i>The Lady of Shalott (1832)</i><br/><br/> I didn't get hooked into Arthurian legends by reading, it was actually the 1995 feature length film <i> First Knight</i> and the performances of Sean Connery, Julia Ormond, and Richard Ger</span>
  <span id="freeText9875580241553863810" style="display:none"><i>There she weaves by night and day <br />A magic web with colours gay. <br />She has heard a whisper say, <br />A curse is on her if she stay <br /> To look down to Camelot. <br />She knows not what the curse may be, <br />And so she weaveth steadily, <br />And little other care hath she, <br /> The Lady of Shalott. </i> Alfred, Lord Tennyson <i>The Lady of Shalott (1832)</i><br /><br /> I didn't get hooked into Arthurian legends by reading, it was actually the 1995 feature length film <i> First Knight</i> and the performances of Sean Connery, Julia Ormond, and Richard Gere that intrigued me to the legend. As per the tragic figure of Elaine, Lady of Shalott. Well, that would be the fault of my first literary character, Anne Shirley (<i> Anne of Green Gables</i>) and her devotion to Tennyson. <i> Song of the Sparrow</i> is told in verse and puts Elaine on the centre stage. Although I am a bit lukewarm on reading almost 400 pages of verse, I did enjoy Lisa Ann Sandell's spin on giving us a full disclosure on Elaine. But there weren't a ton of surprises- Arthur is a good person, but a little blind when it comes to mean girl Guinevere and her affection for playboy Lancelot. Most of the knights make their appearances as well and they come with their own bag of issues. I would still recommend it, but probably wouldn't re-read it again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9875580241553863810" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_40700194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40700194" />
    <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days"><img alt="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420332941p2/395599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40700194?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days">Shannon (Giraffe Days)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780439918497" href="/book/show/3551005-song-of-the-sparrow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=2008">2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=myth">myth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=cover-love">cover-love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40700194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9452703651696810366">I have never had any interest in the Arthurian Legend, and have avoided the many books about it since reading <i>The Sword in the Stone</i> in grade 6 (and winning the school library's book jacket competition with a new cover for it), and struggling through the first fifty pages of <i>The Mists of Avalon</i>. There was even a course at uni that was solely about the legend of King Arthur - which I ran a mile to avoid. I can't really explain what I don't like about the myth, except to say that I don't get the a</span>
  <span id="freeText9452703651696810366" style="display:none">I have never had any interest in the Arthurian Legend, and have avoided the many books about it since reading <i>The Sword in the Stone</i> in grade 6 (and winning the school library's book jacket competition with a new cover for it), and struggling through the first fifty pages of <i>The Mists of Avalon</i>. There was even a course at uni that was solely about the legend of King Arthur - which I ran a mile to avoid. I can't really explain what I don't like about the myth, except to say that I don't get the attraction or why so many people feel the need to write their own versions of it. They also clutter up the fantasy shelves - since it is a myth, a legend, the books wind up there instead of the regular fiction section. I do love Monty Python's <i>The Holy Grail</i> though. Lovely a good piss-take.<br /><br />This book is different, though. It is the story of Elaine of Ascolat, otherwise known as the Lady of Shallot, and it's a reinvigorated, emancipated story. Elaine has always got the short-shrift, suffering under a curse and early death, or dying of a broken heart. In <i>Song of the Sparrow</i>, Sandell tells the story of sixteen year old Elaine, raised since her mother was killed by Picts in the war camp of Arthur, living with her two brothers and her father. <br /><br />The only female there, aside from Arthur's mysterious and wise sister Morgan who sometimes appears, she is friend, nurse and confidant to many of Arthur's men. Her hero is Lancelot, who has been her friend and playmate since she was 12, and whom she loves.<br /><br />But when Arthur takes command of the Britons and Lancelot goes to fetch a nobleman who had at first refused Arthur's leadership, he brings back with him a beautiful young bride for Arthur, Gwynivere - and no one can miss the look of complete adoration on Lancelot's face when he gazes at her.<br /><br />Gwynivere is cold and bitchy and spurns Elaine's offers of friendship while encouraging Lancelot's love. The enmity between them becomes hatred, and only the friendship of one of Arthur's knights, Tristan, can help soothe Elaine's pain. When the army marches to Mount Badon for an offensive attack on the invading Saxons, Elaine resolutely follows them, only to be caught by Saxons when she had nearly reached them - along with Gwynivere, who had followed her in turn.<br /><br />Written in verse, I at first found it a bit weird - it didn't rhyme or even seem to have much of a rhythm, and it could easily have been laid out in regular prose format. However, after a few pages I slipped into the gentle cadences of Elaine's voice, found that there was a rhythm, a song to the words, that they glided through me gracefully and conveyed Elaine's story better than bald prose would have. Narrated in the present tense by Elaine, it carries a sense of immediacy, the language vivid and immersed in nature. The verse has deep roots with the earth, as Elaine is tied to it, as well as the honest emotions of herself and those around her.<br /><br />Elaine is a strong, resourceful character with a decisive will. She is also very endearing and sympathetic and a great Young Adult heroine. No damsel in distress, here. Some fans of the Legend may feel it sacrilegious and too modern a version in its perspective and plotting, but since other stories of Elaine are also products of their times - and written by men - it's only fair. I guess this is why, to answer my own question, so many people feel a need to write their own versions of the legend. Plus, this is a really lovely story, and I can see it making a great film. I may have fallen in love with Tristan a bit.<br /><br />Has it made me want to read more books about King Arthur and his knights? Not really. It has made me more curious about a period of British and European - and Roman - history that I'm hazy on. I studied European history from 1100 onwards, and only dealt with Roman history while studying Ancient Civilisations, so it's a bit of a blank spot for me. I wouldn't mind reading a really good history book on ancient Briton, if I could find one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9452703651696810366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_137374977" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Annalisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/542037-annalisa"><img alt="Annalisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226336540p2/542037.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137374977?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annalisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annalisa" href="/user/show/542037-annalisa">Annalisa</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Annalisa by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/816534-annie">Annie</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/542037-annalisa?shelf=verse">verse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/542037-annalisa?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137374977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11858832101758327981">I've decided I just don't like modern books in verse. There is no cadence to them. Take for instance this stance:<br/><br/>The noise brings me back,<br/>the fearsome noise of swords<br/>striking swords, <br/>a metallic clanging that rings in<br/>my ears, echoing and echoing<br/>the fearsome<br/>din of men<br/>screaming and crying as they<br/>meet the sharp ends of blades.<br/><br/>Why is the fourth line broken after in? There is no rhythm in that. And why is the second to the last line not broken before as they? I'd personally separate echoing and echoin</span>
  <span id="freeText11858832101758327981" style="display:none">I've decided I just don't like modern books in verse. There is no cadence to them. Take for instance this stance:<br /><br />The noise brings me back,<br />the fearsome noise of swords<br />striking swords, <br />a metallic clanging that rings in<br />my ears, echoing and echoing<br />the fearsome<br />din of men<br />screaming and crying as they<br />meet the sharp ends of blades.<br /><br />Why is the fourth line broken after in? There is no rhythm in that. And why is the second to the last line not broken before as they? I'd personally separate echoing and echoing too. There are no examples in this stance of the words Sandell chose to set apart, but most of them lacked the power to require pause or emphasis. You pause at the end of a line, there should by rhythm, significance to those breaks. It gets frustrating to read a book with all these breaks that are insignificant and pointless. It detracts from some of the beautiful imagery that Sandell creates.<br /><br />If you're going to take on something as iconic as The Lady of Shalott, and on top of that write it in verse, you better make sure your piece is worthy of comparison to the greats. Your characters can't be weak backdrops that requires legend to define as worthy of a story. As a fan of Arthurian literature, anything that attempts to redefine these historic stories must be strong and powerful and impressive. This was simple, too modern, and just okay.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11858832101758327981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_4951137" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4951137" />
    <a title="Angie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50378-angie"><img alt="Angie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510342996p2/50378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4951137?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angie" href="/user/show/50378-angie">Angie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=retellings">retellings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=novels-in-verse">novels-in-verse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=belovedbookshelf">belovedbookshelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=girls-who-do-things">girls-who-do-things</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4951137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2294967527785080271">Originally reviewed <a href='http://www.angie-ville.com/2012/04/retro-friday-review-song-of-sparrow-by.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>.<br/><br/>So this is a book I've spent a lot of time talking about. Chances are, if you've hung around these parts, you've heard me push it. But I actually read it for the first time way back in the olden days before the blog was, well, what it is now. I read it shortly after it was first published, back in 2007, when I was writing monthly posts, mere collections of mini-reviews. So SONG OF THE SPARROW got shortchanged. The fun thing is lots of friends have read (and reviewed) it </span>
  <span id="freeText2294967527785080271" style="display:none">Originally reviewed <a href="http://www.angie-ville.com/2012/04/retro-friday-review-song-of-sparrow-by.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br /><br />So this is a book I've spent a lot of time talking about. Chances are, if you've hung around these parts, you've heard me push it. But I actually read it for the first time way back in the olden days before the blog was, well, what it is now. I read it shortly after it was first published, back in 2007, when I was writing monthly posts, mere collections of mini-reviews. So SONG OF THE SPARROW got shortchanged. The fun thing is lots of friends have read (and reviewed) it since, and so I was able to trip through their lovely thoughts and remember my own. When I heard about a retelling of Tennyson's "Lady of Shalott," I was so in. I mean, I'm nothing if not up for a good Camelot tale. I could bore you to tears with my obsession with the entire Arthurian legend, but who needs that on a Friday afternoon? The thing is, when I heard this retelling was, like Tennyson's version, told entirely in verse, I was no longer so sure. Truthfully, I tend to like my modern poetry short and to the point. So I did what I often do. I went to the bookstore and read the first page. Then I read the second page. And on through the tenth, at which point I accepted the delicious inevitable and bought the book.<br /><br />Elaine of Ascolat is sixteen years old and alone. The lone woman among an endless encampment of men, she has grown up wild and independent and determined to keep her father and brothers and friends alive. Though she is not allowed to fight alongside them, she works tirelessly in any way she can to keep their spirits alive, to mend both their limbs and their souls between battle forays against the ever-encroaching Saxons. After her mother died, her father brought his two sons and one small daughter to live with the soldiers. And so Elaine's oldest friends are Arthur, Lancelot, Gawain, Tristan. But when Ambrosius Aurelius, dux bellorum, is killed, Elaine must watch the laughing eyes of her friends turn grim with strain and responsibility. She must watch as they rally around Arthur--their new leader. As they reform in their new roles and battle leaders and men. And she must watch as a someone new enters their lives and disrupts their old balance perhaps forever. Gwynivere. Haughty and proud, she ensnares Elaine's friends with seemingly no effort at all. But, though her boys seem entranced, Elaine can see clearly just how much craft Gwynivere puts into the web she casts. And when that web extends to Lancelot, the one she loves most, wild, independent, determined Elaine decides it is time to fight at last.<br /><br />This book. This book set a flock of butterflies free in my stomach on the first page. The writing is that heady blend of urgency, vision, and nostalgia. I loved Sandell's revisionist version of Elaine of Ascolat. I <i>knew</i> there was more to her than her magic web and her love for Lancelot. I just <i>knew</i> there was. Sometimes you meet a character, sometimes over and over through the years, and you know her. In bits and pieces, through various art forms and articulations, I have always felt a kinship with the lily maid. But it took until <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/310364.Lisa_Ann_Sandell" title="Lisa Ann Sandell" rel="nofollow noopener">Lisa Ann Sandell</a> decided to paint her version of her, for me to realize why. All that history you just know is there, the spell, the mirror, knotted web of threads, the loyalty to Lancelot, it all comes together in SONG OF THE SPARROW. Any lover of all things Arthurian will tell you, it can be a life of suffering. A rich life, but a rocky one. This retelling soothes the soul. And the beautiful thing is that, by all accounts, it seems to work for newbies (even those utterly uninterested in the myth) as well as us dedicated fans. And it's because the writing and characterization are strong and sure. I'm so glad this Elaine chose to fight instead of die. I loved beautiful Gwynivere, and the choice she makes. The women in this novel are ace. They inhabit the tale, fleshing it out with life and pain and wanting, and together they are my favorite part about this version. But right up there with the ladies is the way Sandell wove in Tristan and his horrible past. In a genius move, we get a glimpse of Tristan (of Tristan and Isolde fame), and I kind of am of the opinion it should always be this way. Forgive the long passage, but it is the moment Elaine first comes to the camp, and it is a favorite.<br /><blockquote>It was nighttime when we reached the camp.<br />When my mind began making sense<br />of what it saw and heard again.<br />In the torchlight I could see Lavain's face<br />was smeared with dirt,<br />streaked with ash.<br />His eyes were still wide with shock,<br />so white<br />so white<br />against his dirty ash face.<br />He looked like a scared, wild animal.<br />I must have looked the same.<br />Frightened animals.<br />Arthur, younger then,<br />stepped forward,<br />caught my father in his<br />arms in an embrace.<br />Then Tirry.<br />He pressed little Lavain's shoulder,<br />then put his hands on my hair,<br />petting, stroking.<br />And I felt safe,<br />a tiny bit,<br />for the first time again.<br /><i>Poor children</i>, he murmured.<br /><i>You are welcome here,<br />in this camp,<br />into this brotherhood.<br />Lavain, someday, no doubt,<br />you will be a fierce fighter.<br />Aye, I can see it in your eyes.<br />But for now, you must take care<br />of your little sister.</i><br />Lavain turned away sullenly,but I alone saw him blink<br />back tears.<br />Arthur looked to me,<br /><i>What a brave girl you are,<br />indeed, I've never met a girl<br />so courageous.<br />There are not any others<br />here to keep you company,<br />but you have a whole army<br />of brothers now.</i><br />He gave me a sad smile and<br />stepped back.<br />Then raven-haired Lancelot came to us,<br />kneeling to look in my eyes.<br />And I felt I was standing in<br />the sunlight, as though<br />his bright gaze alone could warm<br />my frozen insides.<br />He had blankets for Lavain and me.<br />And once more I felt protected.<br />Finally, a young boy who could not<br />have been more than a few years<br />older than Lavain<br />presented me with a doll<br />unevenly sewn of corn husks and rags.<br />He turned to Lavain and placed<br />a wooden sword in his hand.<br />He said his name was Tristan.<br />His golden cat eyes shone in the dark,<br />his mouth downturned, his brow<br />creased as though--<br />as though he knew.</blockquote><br />He said his name was Tristan . . . This one joins <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41424.Beauty" title="Beauty by Robin McKinley" rel="nofollow noopener">Beauty</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13928.Daughter_of_the_Forest__Sevenwaters___1_" title="Daughter of the Forest (Sevenwaters, #1) by Juliet Marillier" rel="nofollow noopener">Daughter of the Forest</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41423.The_Outlaws_of_Sherwood" title="The Outlaws of Sherwood by Robin McKinley" rel="nofollow noopener">The Outlaws of Sherwood</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/266607.Valiant__The_Modern_Faerie_Tales___2_" title="Valiant (The Modern Faerie Tales, #2) by Holly Black" rel="nofollow noopener">Valiant</a> on my most beloved retellings shelf. I love gifting it. I love re-reading it. And I will be doing both for the foreseeable future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2294967527785080271" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_258872758" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/258872758" />
    <a title="Reynje" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5590906-reynje"><img alt="Reynje" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473722441p2/5590906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/258872758?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reynje" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reynje" href="/user/show/5590906-reynje">Reynje</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer258872758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10897456922171242635">I read Song of the Sparrow last year, but it’s only now that I’m posting my rating and review because, in truth, I was nervous. <br/><br/>I have good friends who all seemed to fall into passionate, swoony love with Lisa Ann Sandell’s verse interpretation of the Lady of Shallott. And I.. did not. So I held off. Did I really want to be the lone lukewarm drop in the bucket of adoration? And maybe it was me. Maybe I was just a philistine with no literary taste. While reviewers and critics alike praise this bo</span>
  <span id="freeText10897456922171242635" style="display:none">I read Song of the Sparrow last year, but it’s only now that I’m posting my rating and review because, in truth, I was nervous. <br /><br />I have good friends who all seemed to fall into passionate, swoony love with Lisa Ann Sandell’s verse interpretation of the Lady of Shallott. And I.. did not. So I held off. Did I really want to be the lone lukewarm drop in the bucket of adoration? And maybe it was me. Maybe I was just a philistine with no literary taste. While reviewers and critics alike praise this book, I just didn’t feel the magic. <br /><br />After some thought, I suspect that verse novels are just not my particular <i>“thing”</i>. Like when my friends are talking about how aesthetically pleasing Gerard Butler is and I’m just sitting there with my <i>”Um, what?"</i> face on. Or when people say that rocket is the best salad green, and I say “I hate rocket” and everyone goes quiet. (I have a complicated and ridiculous relationship with salad leaves, but that's a rant for another day). I feel a similar awkwardness in admitting that I don’t enjoy reading verse novels. <br /><br />As I read Song of the Sparrow, I remember having two very distinct, recurring thoughts. <br /><br />(1) <i>“I wish this story was written in prose, not poetry”</i> and <br />(2) <i>“I just want to be finished with this”</i>. <br /><br />I’d flick forward to check how many pages I had left to read – not a good sign. I was loathe to give up and DNF it, because I thought that maybe, just maybe, I’d reach some incredible passage and see the light. Become a verse convert. Write a review testifying to the brilliance of this book. <br /><br />But I can’t. <br /><br />Don’t get me wrong – I think this is a good book. Very good, even. I enjoyed Sandell’s particular take on the characters and the way she wove threads of the original legends, myths and historical texts into her own richly imagined story. This more “realistic” take on King Arthur, Lancelot et al is definitely to my taste. Likewise, I appreciate that Sandell wrote strong, interesting female characters who took initiative and demonstrated courage. I liked the way she developed the relationship between Elaine and Gwynivere, from initial coldness and apparent antagonism to mutual respect and friendship. On the other hand, I found the romance <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[between Elaine and Tristan <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> very sweet, but not particularly compelling. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Although, yes, I liked Tristan :)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />It may just be the history geek in me, but I wanted more detail. More battle, more blood, more description, more dialogue. Basically the elements that would appear in a work of historical fiction/fantasy, but don’t necessarily lend themselves to the flow and syntax of a story told in verse. To be honest, I found the rhythm of the poetry distracting rather than immersive. <br /><br />I can see why people adore this book, and if you’re a fan of verse novels, I wouldn’t hesitate to recommend this one. <br /><br />As for me? Well I’m sorry, verse novels (and rocket. And Gerard Butler.) It’s not you. It really <i>is</i> just me. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10897456922171242635" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_8440267" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8440267" />
    <a title="Emily Cassady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395486-emily-cassady"><img alt="Emily Cassady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1193827583p2/395486.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8440267?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily Cassady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily Cassady" href="/user/show/395486-emily-cassady">Emily Cassady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">YA readers, those interested in Arthur and the Knights</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8440267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15952610747265389265">This prose-style novel ends with the disclaimer that the author has completely used artistic license based on semi-fact. I love that the author spun a fanciful and romantic tale and ended it with responsible tone and further research notes. <br/>Sandell, before beginning her tale, includes the poem by Tennyson entitled “The Lady of Shalott”. This poem sets the tone for the main character who Sandell speculates, is actually the true Lady. <br/>She then spins her tale of Arthur and the Round Table. Of Gwyni</span>
  <span id="freeText15952610747265389265" style="display:none">This prose-style novel ends with the disclaimer that the author has completely used artistic license based on semi-fact. I love that the author spun a fanciful and romantic tale and ended it with responsible tone and further research notes. <br />Sandell, before beginning her tale, includes the poem by Tennyson entitled “The Lady of Shalott”. This poem sets the tone for the main character who Sandell speculates, is actually the true Lady. <br />She then spins her tale of Arthur and the Round Table. Of Gwynivere, Lancelot, Tristan (of Tristan and Isolde fame), Merlin, and Elaine (The Lady of Shalott). In it she includes intrigues of all types. Her prose style excellently frames up words that call for pause and digestion. It is masterful.<br />The story’s main character is Elaine. Elaine lost her mother early on in the ongoing war over Briton. Since she was a little girl she has followed her father and brothers around from encampment to encampment, living among the men, serving as a healer, mender, etc… Elaine interacts with each of the afore mentioned characters and <br />Elaine is getting older. Lancelot notices Elaine and she quite easily falls for him, or at least acquires a crush. Lancelot leads her on a bit, gives her hope, but then has to ride out on a mission for Arthur, the new king.<br />The mission is to bring back Arthur’s new betrothed queen, Gwynivere, and along the way, as legend goes, Lancelot falls irrevocably in love with Gwynivere.<br />Once back at camp, Elaine feels betrayed at Lancelot’s change of heart. To top it off Gwynivere has a far superior attitude and while Elaine and Gwyn should have shared a sister bond at this camp filled with all men, they now have a rivalry.<br />When Elaine slips off to follow Arthur’s camp to war, Gwyn follows. And when Elaine falls into enemy clutches, it is up to Gwyn to save her. The two girls must hatch an elaborate plan to save the men that they love and, in turn, form an irrevocable bond. <br />With several sub-plots present and the whimsical language of chivalry, this novel is a great romantic read that will entertain students who like Arthurian lore. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15952610747265389265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review8440267">

<div id="review_150366734" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150366734" />
    <a title="Holly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/398650-holly"><img alt="Holly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455727436p2/398650.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150366734?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Holly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Holly" href="/user/show/398650-holly">Holly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Holly by: </span>
      Angie
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/398650-holly?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/398650-holly?shelf=novels-in-verse">novels-in-verse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150366734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13494376476379220703">Red-headed Elaine of Ascolat has been the sole girl at Arthur’s war camp for as long as she can remember. Although she misses her mother and dislikes all the washing and mending Elaine wouldn’t trade living with her father and brothers and being part of the military for a quieter life. There, as a healer, her job matters. She may not have fine clothes but she knows how to take care of herself and more importantly how to find and prescribe the herbs that can save the wounded. Her life would be co</span>
  <span id="freeText13494376476379220703" style="display:none">Red-headed Elaine of Ascolat has been the sole girl at Arthur’s war camp for as long as she can remember. Although she misses her mother and dislikes all the washing and mending Elaine wouldn’t trade living with her father and brothers and being part of the military for a quieter life. There, as a healer, her job matters. She may not have fine clothes but she knows how to take care of herself and more importantly how to find and prescribe the herbs that can save the wounded. Her life would be complete if only Lancelot could see her as more than a tomboy. Just when she seems to be making headway another girl named Gwynivere arrives and Lancelot only has eyes for her. Fair skin, perfectly coiffed hair, soft hands and all seem no match for Elaine. That is until both girls are caught by the enemy. Here is Elaine’s chance to shine, but will the sparrow find its song before being trapped for good?<br /><br />This is the third novel in verse I’ve ever read and wow, is the writing gorgeous. The words just wash over you they are so fluid. It seems verse couldn’t be more fitting for Lisa Ann Sandell’s take on Arthurian legend and Alfred Tennyson’s poem, “Lady of Shallott”. This winsome passage was one of my favorites:<br /><br /> I wish I could go back to that time,<br /> When my mother would smile<br /> The gentle smile that told me,<br /> All is right and well.<br /><br /> Back to that time when I was<br /> Young<br /> And loved<br /> And safe.<br /> When we were all safe.<br /><br /> That things change,<br /> That people change<br /> And die,<br /> That we grow older,<br /> That life brings the unexpected,<br /> The unwanted,<br /> Oh,<br /> Some days it fills me with<br /> A measure of lightness, for<br /> I will be a woman soon.<br /><br /> But other days,<br /> The very thought<br /> of growing older,<br /> of not being that small girl<br /> who danced over river rocks,<br /> whose brothers held her hands,<br /> whose mother lived,<br /> the very thought of it<br /> crushes me,<br /> till it is stopped,<br /> by the world<br /> outside<br /> my memories.<br /><br />I’ve never been hugely interested in Arthurian legend beyond JW Waterhouse’s beautiful Lady of Shallott paintings but I enjoyed Sandell’s revisionist version. Much more than another King Arthur retelling THE SONG OF THE SPARROW is its own, unique story with a Elaine as a very strong, assertive protagonist rather than the flimsy damsel of legend. A good mix of the traditional and modern female roles, Elaine is not willing to sit around, making the mothers of feisty heroines everywhere proud. Likewise modern “mean girls” would accept beautiful Gwynivere in her mean-spirited, snotty treatment of Elaine. Even Tristan with his shameful past is very interestingly cast here as Elaine’s witty friend. Such dimensional characters, lovely metaphors, and vivid imagery make THE SONG OF THE SPARROW a truly stunning read. Highly recommended to fans of well done retellings and verse novels alike.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13494376476379220703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1317406980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17755903193746662028">What can I say? I love this book.<br/><br/>Are the characters' behaviors accurate depictions of how the historic/mythological figures they represent would behave? Probably not (especially the ladies)... but I love it. Is the conflict that drives the plot resolved very quickly and without much effort? Sure... but I love it. Is the setting beautiful and whimsical and just a little magical? You bet your ass it is, and I love it. <br/><br/>My love for this book is one part nostalgia for the story that got me into Arthu</span>
  <span id="freeText17755903193746662028" style="display:none">What can I say? I love this book.<br /><br />Are the characters' behaviors accurate depictions of how the historic/mythological figures they represent would behave? Probably not (especially the ladies)... but I love it. Is the conflict that drives the plot resolved very quickly and without much effort? Sure... but I love it. Is the setting beautiful and whimsical and just a little magical? You bet your ass it is, and I love it. <br /><br />My love for this book is one part nostalgia for the story that got me into Arthurian legend, one part love for the tropes it uses, and two parts just plain fun. Objectively it's not a masterpiece of fiction<br /><br />... but I love it. And so I stand by my 4/5 stars.<br /><br />(Also I can't believe it's been THREE years since I've read it?? I could've sworn I'd read it at least once more between June of 2015 and now...)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10209084066392521684">I loved this book. I was a bit worried about the poem structure and I was also worried because it was about Lady Elaine.   <span id="freeText10209084066392521684" style="display:none">I loved this book. I was a bit worried about the poem structure and I was also worried because it was about Lady Elaine. But I really could admire Elaine and her courage. And I could follow the book just fine. I really liked that the men were very chilvaric and honorable. The few women that were in the story were not pathetic and swooning all the time. They actually played a big part in Arthur's success. Elaine helped in a realistic way during that period in time. After I finished reading it I was smiling from cheek to cheek. Although I think that this book is more for girls, which is fine with me. <br /><br />Hope to read it again.</span>

<div id="review_151988803" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151988803" />
    <a title="Chachic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3294750-chachic"><img alt="Chachic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502809275p2/3294750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151988803?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chachic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chachic" href="/user/show/3294750-chachic">Chachic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780439918497" href="/book/show/3551005-song-of-the-sparrow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3294750-chachic?shelf=best-of-2011">best-of-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3294750-chachic?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151988803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1561131540769023787">Originally posted <a href='http://chachic.wordpress.com/2011/03/04/song-of-the-sparrow-by-lisa-ann-sandell/' rel='nofollow noopener'>here.</a><br/><br/><em>Song of the Sparrow</em> is based on Alfred Lord Tennyson's <em>The Lady of Shalott</em>, an Arthurian poem about Elaine of Ascolat. I've never read a novel in verse before and I thought it would be a good idea to start with this one because I like the premise. I don't read a lot of Arthurian tales either although I remember reading <em>Le Morte d'Arthur</em> for English back in high school and I love <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/52320.Elizabeth_E_Wein' rel='nofollow noopener'>Elizabeth E. Wein's</a> books. When I saw an inexpensive used copy from <a href='http://juliesari2store.multiply.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Julie's Sari-Sari Store</a>, I </span>
  <span id="freeText1561131540769023787" style="display:none">Originally posted <a href="http://chachic.wordpress.com/2011/03/04/song-of-the-sparrow-by-lisa-ann-sandell/" rel="nofollow noopener">here.</a><br /><br /><em>Song of the Sparrow</em> is based on Alfred Lord Tennyson's <em>The Lady of Shalott</em>, an Arthurian poem about Elaine of Ascolat. I've never read a novel in verse before and I thought it would be a good idea to start with this one because I like the premise. I don't read a lot of Arthurian tales either although I remember reading <em>Le Morte d'Arthur</em> for English back in high school and I love <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/52320.Elizabeth_E_Wein" rel="nofollow noopener">Elizabeth E. Wein's</a> books. When I saw an inexpensive used copy from <a href="http://juliesari2store.multiply.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Julie's Sari-Sari Store</a>, I bought it right away. Thanks to <a href="http://booksandmagazines.multiply.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Celina</a> for the heads up on where I could find a copy.<br /><br />I was swept away by the beautiful writing in <em>Song of the Sparrow</em>. Maybe it's because of the verse format but it felt like I was reading a fairy tale instead of a historical fiction book. I was easily immersed in the story and I knew right from the start that Elaine and I would get along just fine. Elaine is a girl stuck in a world full of men and she can be described as "one of the boys". Her father brought her to Arthur's camp when her mother died and she's been there ever since. Her father and her two brothers fight alongside the knights of Arthur and she has great respect for all of them. As the only lady in their camp, Elaine's sewing and healing skills are in great demand. She doesn't mind because she's friends with most of the men in their camp and she enjoys the freedom that her lifestyle allows. What I loved about Elaine's character in this retelling is that she manages to show her strength without picking up a sword or fighting in a battle like other fantasy heroines (not that I don't love them). Elaine's infatuation with Lancelot is an integral part of the story because that's what she's famous for but I liked how the author provided a background for it - how Lancelot was always there whenever Elaine was lonely as a child and how he comes to the rescue the few times that Elaine needs help. It isn't a tragic kind of love, which was how it was portrayed by other writers.<br /><br />I don't read much poetry so I was afraid that I wouldn't be able to relate to this one but surprise, surprise, the pages just flew by. To get a feel of the writing, check out <a href="http://www.lisaannsandell.com/sotsexcerpt.html" rel="nofollow noopener">the excerpt available in Lisa Ann Sandell's website.</a> The story provided not just a clear picture of Elaine but of other well-known characters like Gwynivere, Lancelot, Tristan and Arthur. I loved seeing how Elaine interacted with all of them, even Gwynivere who is everything Elaine isn't - beautiful, ladylike, cold and cruel. I made an excellent decision when I chose <em>Song of the Sparrow</em> as my first novel in verse because now I'm curious about books written in a similar format. I wonder if other novels in verse are as lovely as this one. I highly recommend this to fans of Arthurian tales, retellings or novels in verse. Or maybe I should just say, read this if you want to fall in love with an exquisite retelling about Elaine of Ascolat, the Lady of Shalott.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1561131540769023787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15388087884375377093">My Arthurian knowledge is very limited. I really only know what I have picked up in snippets from other books or television; which honestly wasn’t very much. I had no idea that Elaine had even existed, and I only knew of Tristan from that movie with Heath Ledger; which I hadn’t watched :D.<br/>Okay so long story short, this was a fantastic first introduction for me. The pace was fast and easy, it flowed really well; and despite my worry that I wouldn’t connect with the characters because of the forma</span>
  <span id="freeText15388087884375377093" style="display:none">My Arthurian knowledge is very limited. I really only know what I have picked up in snippets from other books or television; which honestly wasn’t very much. I had no idea that Elaine had even existed, and I only knew of Tristan from that movie with Heath Ledger; which I hadn’t watched :D.<br />Okay so long story short, this was a fantastic first introduction for me. The pace was fast and easy, it flowed really well; and despite my worry that I wouldn’t connect with the characters because of the format of the story, there was no problems there.<br />I love Elaine, she is such a wonderful woman! She has yet to come into her own; which is understandable considering she has been the only girl in a camp full of men. She feels almost childish in the beginning flitting around camp and pining over Lancelot. I was really worried for her considering I know where Lancelot’s devotion ends up! But she tumbles through and comes out none the worse for wear. I loved how she stood up to Gwyneviere and ended up being the awesome badass woman she is! Major kudos to Elaine!<br />The verse style of writing took a little while for me to get into as I am not really familiar with it. However, it really didn’t take long to get into it. I don’t think it took away from the story and the author was able to get across the emotions as well. Spectacular job! I highly recommend! <br /></span>

<div id="review_115658716" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115658716" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115658716?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=arthurian">arthurian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=based-on-myth-saga-etc">based-on-myth-saga-etc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=children-s-and-ya">children-s-and-ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115658716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17776017790969032781">This ended up not really being my thing, and I mostly skimmed it. It's laid out like poetry, but I'm not sure it's really written as poetry. I'm not the type of person that dismisses free verse, or anything like that, but it didn't flow or condense the images in the way that poetry typically does -- it feels like staccato prose, instead. A quick example:<br/><br/><i>He takes a deep breath.<br/>And I loved being your hero.<br/>But that day, that day when you<br/>offered yourself to me, I was shocked, and I was angry with th</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17776017790969032781" style="display:none">This ended up not really being my thing, and I mostly skimmed it. It's laid out like poetry, but I'm not sure it's really written as poetry. I'm not the type of person that dismisses free verse, or anything like that, but it didn't flow or condense the images in the way that poetry typically does -- it feels like staccato prose, instead. A quick example:<br /><br /><i>He takes a deep breath.<br />And I loved being your hero.<br />But that day, that day when you<br />offered yourself to me, I was shocked, and I was angry with the<br />world, drowning in self-pity.<br />I dismissed you as a child who could never understand.<br />But now, I suspect you understood<br />better than anyone.<br />A bitter smile that does not<br />reach his eyes twists his mouth.</i><br /><br />Maybe it's just that those descriptions strike me as very prosey. If I wrote it in prose, it might sound a bit like that. If I wrote it in poetry, it'd be nothing like.<br /><br />In any case, I just couldn't settle down and enjoy this as a story because of that, and I'm not sure I like this choice of interpretation, either. Her eventual lover makes little sense, in the context of Arthurian canon -- famously adaptive as it is -- and I couldn't get behind that, either.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1518162944338431434">Reviewed by Holly for TeensReadToo.com<br/><br/>Sixteen-year-old Elaine of Ascolat is amazingly beautiful with her long red hair and her soft natural face. Living in an army camp full of all guys, Elaine figures the handsome Lancelot to be her true love. Until her troubles and daydreams get the best of her, when even prettier Gwynivere arrives at the camp and is immediatley drawn to Lancelot - even though she is engaged to Arthur. <br/><br/>Gwynivere's mean remarks but beautiful outer self makes Elaine jealous enou</span>
  <span id="freeText1518162944338431434" style="display:none">Reviewed by Holly for TeensReadToo.com<br /><br />Sixteen-year-old Elaine of Ascolat is amazingly beautiful with her long red hair and her soft natural face. Living in an army camp full of all guys, Elaine figures the handsome Lancelot to be her true love. Until her troubles and daydreams get the best of her, when even prettier Gwynivere arrives at the camp and is immediatley drawn to Lancelot - even though she is engaged to Arthur. <br /><br />Gwynivere's mean remarks but beautiful outer self makes Elaine jealous enough to play a cruel prank on her, but when her friends and family leave her to go fight the Saxons, her heart tells her to pack a bag and go with them to heal the wounded. Shot by arrows and cut by a knife, Elaine gains both friendship and confidence in herself. <br /><br />This exciting book by Lisa Ann Sandell makes you feel like you are back in olden times, right there fighting with Elaine. This is a VERY well-written book and I recommend it to all. A great read! </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2915661220?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristina" href="/user/show/35928172-kristina">Kristina</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2915661220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14035647635948485530">This fast-paced novel-in-verse has been sitting on my TBR shelf for ages! While lyrically poetic and historically-based, I found myself bogged down in the novel’s numerous cliches and juvenile language. The heroine is fabulous and brave and the knights are handsome and dashing, but overall the story felt somewhat rushed and overdone. I love that the author took Elaine’s story and removed the distress from the damsel, however, she also completely rewrote the story to make it more like a harlequin</span>
  <span id="freeText14035647635948485530" style="display:none">This fast-paced novel-in-verse has been sitting on my TBR shelf for ages! While lyrically poetic and historically-based, I found myself bogged down in the novel’s numerous cliches and juvenile language. The heroine is fabulous and brave and the knights are handsome and dashing, but overall the story felt somewhat rushed and overdone. I love that the author took Elaine’s story and removed the distress from the damsel, however, she also completely rewrote the story to make it more like a harlequin romance. “The Lady of Shalott” is one of my all-time favorite poems and I honestly think this novel would have been exponentially better if the author kept the story true to its original form with all its mystery and myth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14035647635948485530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_187823316" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/187823316" />
    <a title="Tina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1552211-tina"><img alt="Tina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333881094p2/1552211.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/187823316?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tina" href="/user/show/1552211-tina">Tina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=verse">verse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer187823316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4263479030796252196"><strong>Original post at <a href='http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/song-of-the-sparrow' rel='nofollow noopener'>One More Page</a></strong><br/><br/>This year is the year of novels in verse for me, and I have been trying to keep one on my TBR in case of a need for a quick read. <em>
  <strong>Song of the Sparrow</strong>
</em> by Lisa Ann Sandell is one of those books that hovered by my radar but I never really got because...well I'm not sure anymore. But anyway, when I saw a copy of this for swap in one of our Goodreads meet-ups, I got it immediately (with cackles of delight because I got it first -- then again I'm not sure if anyone el</span>
  <span id="freeText4263479030796252196" style="display:none"><strong>Original post at <a href="http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/song-of-the-sparrow" rel="nofollow noopener">One More Page</a></strong><br /><br />This year is the year of novels in verse for me, and I have been trying to keep one on my TBR in case of a need for a quick read. <em>
  <strong>Song of the Sparrow</strong>
</em> by Lisa Ann Sandell is one of those books that hovered by my radar but I never really got because...well I'm not sure anymore. But anyway, when I saw a copy of this for swap in one of our Goodreads meet-ups, I got it immediately (with cackles of delight because I got it first -- then again I'm not sure if anyone else wanted this more than I did).<br /><br />Anyway, I haven't read any book about Arthur or anything related to this legend, but I am a bit familiar because of all those Camelot-related cartoons and of course, Merlin. I wasn't familiar with any other characters there, though, so this book came as a surprise and a bit of a crash course with the legend. Elaine of Ascolat is one of the two girls in the military camp where Arthur and his men stay to fight for Britain. Elaine's only other companion is Arthur's sister, who she loves but can't talk to about her secret: that she is secretly in love with Lancelot, Arthur's right hand man. Then another girl joins them in the camp, Gwynivere, and Elaine suddenly felt some competition for Lancelot's affection.<br /><br />I wasn't really afraid that I won't like this book, but I also wasn't expecting to really like it so much. Despite its historical/mythical nature, the passages in this book were very easy to read. It was easy to slip into Elaine's world and imagine how it is to be the only girl in a camp of men, and treat the guys as old friends and brothers who you know will also watch out for you. I could also easily feel her frustration of not having a girl friend in the camp and even more so Elaine's jealousy and anger when attention goes from her to Gwynivere. It may be the book's format that somehow made the book a little more romantic than I expected it to be -- poetry does have its merits in that department. Plus, I wasn't expecting the romantic twist in the story, and how it was resolved really made me smile. Then again, I don't know if the twist here is really a part of the Arthur legend, so maybe I'm the only one who's surprised.<br /><br />A favorite passage:<br /><blockquote><em>But I believe, </em>I continue, <em>I know what true love is -- or what it should be.</em><br /><br /><em>What should it be? </em>Tristan asks, his voice soft now.<br /><br />It should begin with friendship, I think.<br /><br />Suddenly I cannot look at him.<br /><br /><em>It should begin with friendship and truly knowing<br />who a person is, knowing his flaws and hopes<br />and strengths and fears, knowing all of it.<br />And admiring and caring for -- loving<br />the person because of all those things.</em><br /><br />(p. 366)</blockquote><br /><em>
  <strong>Song of the Sparrow </strong>
</em>is a lovely book, and this has made me more curious about more Arthur stories. If you're looking for a quick and yes, a little bit romantic read, then pick up a copy of this book. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4263479030796252196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_59125603" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59125603" />
    <a title="Adele" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2108919-adele"><img alt="Adele" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515039432p2/2108919.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59125603?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adele" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adele" href="/user/show/2108919-adele">Adele</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59125603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18081160900646871701">I am usually adverse to verse but having read two amazing verse novels this week, I am about to pull an about face. Song of the Sparrow was an absolute joy to read - lyrical, poetic, inspiring and wrenching. Drawing upon the stories of Arthur, Lancelot and Merlin, Sandell has strongly integrated the inspiration for Tennyson's poem, the Lady of Shalott, into the mix. Elaine (the "Lady" in question) has been raised in the war camps of 5th century Britain, by her widowed father amongst battle weary</span>
  <span id="freeText18081160900646871701" style="display:none">I am usually adverse to verse but having read two amazing verse novels this week, I am about to pull an about face. Song of the Sparrow was an absolute joy to read - lyrical, poetic, inspiring and wrenching. Drawing upon the stories of Arthur, Lancelot and Merlin, Sandell has strongly integrated the inspiration for Tennyson's poem, the Lady of Shalott, into the mix. Elaine (the "Lady" in question) has been raised in the war camps of 5th century Britain, by her widowed father amongst battle weary men. She's loved and respected by these men whilst operating within the confines of what was expected of woman of the day - washing and mending.<br /><br />It reads like an absolute dream. The phrasing is so fluid, so rhythmic, that it rolls off of the page and seeps into your consciousness. Elaine is a strong character, aware of her obligations, supremely concerned for the welfare of those that she loves and in possession of great courage. She's a character that is immediately relatable, likeable and enviable. I personally loved the depiction of Gwynivere as a shrew preying on the attentions of men. She's the one character of Arthur lore that I have always found tedious and while she's initially portrayed as a 5th century 'mean girl', there is also surprising depths to be found in her. This can be said of all the characters, both those that are familiar and new, they are constructed to be both complex and clear in motivation. It's the epitome of exact phrasing and plotting within a story while taking you on a winding story.<br /><br />Despite the fantastic economy of words in this novel, the characterisation and plotting, this novel presents many themes and adventure for the readers to delve into. Sandell explores grief, family unity, gender roles, the nature of love, friendship, infatuation, duty, courage and sacrifice all while grounding Elaine's story with her obvious love of verse and legend.<br /><br />Divine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18081160900646871701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1701342722" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1701342722" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/940959-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508264786p2/940959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1701342722?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/940959-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1701342722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17225358692816338600">I ran into a sweet little twelve year old at a yard sale, and because it's my favorite thing to ask kids, I asked what her favorite book was. I was happy to find that she was a book lover, and she let me give her a whole list of books she should read this summer. Anyway, at the end of the conversation, she said she wanted to loan me this book to read, and I'm always going to take a book recommendation from a kid who takes the time to talk books with me, so I read it.<br/><br/>I have to admit the only thin</span>
  <span id="freeText17225358692816338600" style="display:none">I ran into a sweet little twelve year old at a yard sale, and because it's my favorite thing to ask kids, I asked what her favorite book was. I was happy to find that she was a book lover, and she let me give her a whole list of books she should read this summer. Anyway, at the end of the conversation, she said she wanted to loan me this book to read, and I'm always going to take a book recommendation from a kid who takes the time to talk books with me, so I read it.<br /><br />I have to admit the only thing I liked about the poem-style writing was that it made the huge book go by faster. It didn't really read like poetry (or maybe I didn't take the time to read it like poetry), so all it really did for me is chop up the sentences into fragments that made it difficult to focus and hard to get involved in the story. The story was predictable and really not very engrossing. Almost nothing even happened. So I wouldn't personally recommend this book, but I do love that a little girl liked it enough to loan it to me, and it was worth reading it just for that</span>
  <a data-text-id="17225358692816338600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_182961829" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182961829" />
    <a title="Kayla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3119530-kayla"><img alt="Kayla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420612320p2/3119530.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182961829?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kayla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kayla" href="/user/show/3119530-kayla">Kayla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=retold-fairy-tale">retold-fairy-tale</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=written-in-verse">written-in-verse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=books-i-love">books-i-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=borrowed-at-library">borrowed-at-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3119530-kayla?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182961829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8802396074154525701">I was assigned to read this book as part of the YA Best Overlooked Book Battle 2011, hosted by Alyssa over at The Shady Glade. At first I wasn't sure what to think; I'm just starting to get into historical fiction, and this didn't sound like something I'd ache to pick up on my own. But by giving it a chance, I was pleasantly surprised.<br/><br/>Song of the Sparrow follows Elaine, the only girl in an army camp full of men. She's grown up there, away from the limitations that were enforced upon women at tha</span>
  <span id="freeText8802396074154525701" style="display:none">I was assigned to read this book as part of the YA Best Overlooked Book Battle 2011, hosted by Alyssa over at The Shady Glade. At first I wasn't sure what to think; I'm just starting to get into historical fiction, and this didn't sound like something I'd ache to pick up on my own. But by giving it a chance, I was pleasantly surprised.<br /><br />Song of the Sparrow follows Elaine, the only girl in an army camp full of men. She's grown up there, away from the limitations that were enforced upon women at that time, free to roam as she pleased, though still not allowed to fight for her country. As a result, she's often left behind, alone, as everyone she knows and loves marches off into battle.<br /><br />I loved that this novel was written in verse. I didn't know that until I began reading, and verse books are something of a guilty pleasure for me. The smooth way the lines flowed, the way each thing that came up was so beautifully described through Elaine's perspective, kept me coming back for more, wanting to read on and on. I couldn't get enough of it.<br /><br />The characters in this book, though the minor ones dimmed in comparison to Elaine, were great. Each held true to their own purpose, and won me over, whether they were good or evil. I liked reading about how Arthur and the other knights were brotherly toward Elaine, working for her best interests and trying to protect her. And I loved how she in turn wanted to protect them, though sometimes this meant disobeying them and doing dangerous things.<br /><br />The legend of King Arthur is one that I know well, though never before have I read something like this. It was a refreshing take, told from a female perspective, and actually made the women heroic, for once, instead of having the knights take all of the glory. That little 'girl power' addition fit in nicely.<br /><br />All in all, this book was practically perfect to me. I read through it very quickly, loved every moment of it, and wish there was more. The ending was brilliant, the characters witty and captivating. I highly recommend Song of the Sparrow, even to those that do not normally read historical fiction. I don't, and this only encourages me to read more. Though I may be disappointed; I don't know what can live up to the standards this has set. I give it 5/5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8802396074154525701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
